ہے یا اس کو بغیر مہلت کے اختیار دے دیا جائے گا ؟

(۳) اگر ایسی عورت کو مہلت دی جائے گی تو اس کا شمار کب سے ہوگا ، دعوئی کرنے اور معاملہ پیش کرنے کے وقت سے یا گم ہونے کے وقت سے یا حاکم (شرعی) کے فیصلہ کرنے کے بعد سے ؟

(۳) کیا مفقود (لاپتہ شخص کی بیوی فرخ نکاح میں خود مختار ہے یا اس کے لیے قاضی کا فیصلہ ہونے کی شرط ہے ؟ اور نکاح فسخ ہونے کی کیا صورت ہو سکتی ہے۔

(۳) اگر قاضی کا فیصلہ شرط ہے تو کیا قاضی پر بھی یہ لازم ہے کہ پہلے مفقود کی خود تفتیش اور تلاش کرے جب اس کو نا امیدی ہو جائے تو اُس وقت بیوی کو کوئی مہلت وغیرہ دے یا عورت اور اس کے اولیاء کو تلاش کر لینا کافی ہے؟

(۵) جن علاقوں میں شرعی قامتی موجود نہیں جیسے ہندوستان وغیرہ تو وہاں پر اس کی کیا صورت ہے ؟

مفقود کا حکم دار الحرب اور دارالاسلام میں برابر ہے یا متخلف ؟ اگر مختلف ہے تو پھر ہندوستان جیسے ممالک جن میں کروڑوں مسلمان آباد ہیں وہ دارالاسلام سمجھے جائیں گے ۔

والسلام

لے فسخ نکاح سے اس جگہ فسخ اصطلاحی مراد نہیں۔ بلکہ محاورات اردو کے موافق فسخ کا لفظ اختیار کیا گیا ۔ اور بغرض تقسیم عوام اس رسالہ میں اکثر مواقع میں لفظ فسخ ہی کا اطلاق کیا گیا ہے ۱۲ متہ

الجواب
منجانب مفتیان ،مالکیہ پہلے سوال کا جواب
(1) مفقود کی بیوی کے لیے مالکیہ کے نزدیک مفقود کے نکاح سے علیحدہ ہونے کی دارالاسلام میں تو یہ صورت ہے کہ عورت قاضی کی عدالت میں معاملہ پیش کرے اور شہر بھی گواہی کے ذریعہ یہ ثابت کرے کہ میرا نکاح فلاں شخص ہوا تھا ، اگر نکاح کے موقعہ کے گواہ موجود نہ ہوں تو اس معاملہ میں شہادت با تاسع (یعنی عام شہرت پر بھی کافی ہے یعنی عام شہرت کی بناء پر بھی گواہی دی جا سکتی ہے ۔ كما في المنتفى للباجي المالكي رصفحه ۲۰:۳ جلد ۵ كتاب الاقضية (فرع) واما النكاح فقى العلبة سحنون قال جل اصحابنا يقولون في النكاح اذا استنشر خبره في الجيران ان فلانا تزوج فلانة وسمع الزفاف فله ان يشهدان فلانة زوجة فلان -
اُس کے بعد قاضی خود بھی مفقود کی تحقیق و تلاش کرے اور جب پینہ ملنے سے مایوسی ہو جائے تو عورت کو چار سال تک مزید انتظار کا حکم کرے پھر اگر ان چار سال میں بھی مفقود کا پتہ نہ چلے تو مفقود کو اس چار سال کی مدت ختم ہونے پر مردہ تصور کیا جائے گا، اور نیز ان چار سال کے ختم ہونے کے بعد چار ماہ دس دن عدت وفات گزار کر عورت کو دوسری جگہ نکاح کرنے کا اختیار ہوگا اور اب چار سال گزرنے کے بعد دوبارہ قاضی کی عدالت میں درخواست دینا اور عدت وفات کے لیے فیصلہ حاصل کرنا مالکیہ کے نزدیک ضروری نہیں ۔ بلکہ قاضی کا فیصلہ صرف پہلی مرتبہ مدت دینے کے وقت ضروری ہے۔
كما صرح بذلك في شرح الدردير (صفحه جلدا) حيث قال الخليل
Is it that the woman will be given a period or will she be given the option immediately? (3) If such a woman is given a period, from when will it be counted: from the time of claiming and presenting the case, or from the time of disappearance, or after the decision of the (shari) judge? (3) Is the wife of a missing person independent in invalidating the marriage, or is the judge's decision a condition? And what form can the annulment of marriage take? (3) If the judge's decision is a condition, is it also necessary for the judge to personally investigate and search for the missing person, and when he becomes hopeless, then give some period to the wife, or is it sufficient for the woman and her guardians to search? (5) In areas where there is no shari court, like India etc., what is the ruling there? Is the ruling on the missing person the same in dar al-harb and dar al-islam or different? If different, then countries like India with millions of Muslims – will they be considered dar al-islam? And peace. Note: By 'annulment of marriage' here, technical annulment is not meant; rather, in accordance with Urdu usage, the word 'annulment' has been chosen, and for the general public, in this booklet on most occasions the word 'annulment' is used. Answer: From the Maliki muftis, answer to the first question: (1) For the wife of a missing person, according to the Malikis, the way to separate from the missing person's marriage in dar al-islam is that the woman presents her case in the qadi's court and also establishes through testimony that her marriage to so-and-so took place. If witnesses of the marriage are not present, then testimony based on general repute (shahada bi al-tasa') is sufficient, i.e., testimony can be given on the basis of common knowledge. As in al-Muntaqa al-Baji (5:20:3, Kitab al-Aqdiya): 'Regarding marriage, if news spreads among neighbors that so-and-so married so-and-so and the wedding procession is heard, then one may testify that so-and-so is the wife of so-and-so.' After that, the qadi himself should investigate and search for the missing person. When hopelessness of finding him arises, he orders the woman to wait for four more years. Then if the missing person is not found in these four years, after the end of the four-year period, the missing person is considered dead. Also, after the end of these four years, after observing the iddat of death (four months and ten days), the woman has the option to marry elsewhere. And after the four years have passed, reapplying to the qadi's court and obtaining a decision for the iddat of death is not necessary according to the Malikis. Rather, the qadi's decision is only necessary at the first time of granting the period. As stated in Sharh al-Dardir: 'Al-Khalil said...'
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Maliki school presented in response to a series of questions about the wife of a missing person (mafqud). The author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, is compiling legal stratagems (hila) and here provides the Maliki solution for the wife's remarriage. The first question, partially visible, asks whether the wife is given a waiting period or immediate option. The Maliki answer details a step-by-step procedure: first, the wife must establish her marriage before a qadi through witnesses or, if lacking, through general repute (shahada bi al-tasa'). This latter form of testimony is based on widespread knowledge among the community, as supported by a citation from al-Muntaqa (Maliki source). Next, the qadi conducts his own investigation. When hope is lost, he orders a four-year waiting period. After these four years without trace, the missing person is legally considered dead. The wife then observes the iddat of death (four months and ten days) and may remarry without needing further qadi approval. Notably, the qadi's ruling is required only for the initial waiting period, not for the subsequent iddat. This ruling is exclusive to the Maliki school and is presented as one possible solution within the broader Hanafi framework of the book, intended for those facing such situations in the absence of a comprehensive Hanafi ruling.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A person who is missing, with unknown whereabouts and uncertain life status
شہادت با تاسع (shahada bi al-tasa'): Testimony based on general repute (common knowledge) rather than direct eyewitness
عدت وفات (iddat al-wafat): Waiting period of four months and ten days observed by a widow before remarriage

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maliki school, at which point is the qadi's ruling necessary for the wife of a missing person?
A) Only when she first reports the case
B) After the four-year waiting period
C) At both the initial report and the end of the waiting period
D) Only after she observes iddat

Key Takeaway
The Maliki school provides a structured, qadi-supervised but finite process for the wife of a missing person to remarry, requiring a four-year waiting period and iddat, with only one initial qadi ruling.

Hila Shariyyah — Legal Stratagems
intermediate
7 min
Shahada bi al-Tasa': Maliki Testimony by General Repute
Maliki concept of testimony by general repute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Maliki concept of shahada bi al-tasa' and apply its conditions to establish a marriage when direct witnesses are unavailable.

Core Idea
Maliki law accepts testimony based on widespread public knowledge (shahada bi al-tasa') to prove a marriage when eyewitnesses are absent.

In the Maliki procedure for the wife of a missing person, a crucial first step is proving the marriage. When the marriage ceremony's direct witnesses are lost, Maliki fiqh uniquely accepts testimony based on general repute. This lesson unpacks the definition, conditions, and legal effect of shahada bi al-tasa' as presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Need for Alternative Testimony
In cases where the original witnesses to a marriage are absent or deceased, Maliki law provides an alternative: testimony based on widespread public knowledge.
This is a direct contrast to the Hanafi school, which generally requires two male witnesses or one male and two females for marriage contracts.
2
Define Shahada bi al-Tasa'
Shahada bi al-tasa' is testimony not based on direct observation but on the fact that news of the marriage spread among the community to the point of common knowledge.
The term 'tasa'' literally means 'ninth' but idiomatically refers to general repute or common knowledge.
3
List the Conditions for Acceptance
The text cites al-Muntaqa: the news must have spread among neighbors, and the wedding procession (zifaf) must have been heard. These conditions ensure the knowledge is not merely rumor but widely established.
The conditions protect against false claims based on isolated reports.
4
Understand the Legal Effect
Once the conditions are met, a witness may testify that 'so-and-so is the wife of so-and-so' without direct observation. This testimony is sufficient to establish the marriage in court.
This enables the qadi to proceed with the mafqud case even without formal marriage documents or witnesses.

Hila Shariyyah — Legal Stratagems
intermediate

Hila for the Wife of a Missing Husband: Reconciling Hanafi and Maliki Rulings
Comparative ruling with inter-madhhab solution for wife of missing husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a hila for the wife of a missing husband that satisfies both Hanafi and Maliki conditions, and evaluate how the ruling differs between dar al-Islam and dar al-harb.

Core Idea
A hila can reconcile Hanafi and Maliki rulings: seek a qadi's death ruling after the waiting period.

The passage addresses the plight of a wife whose husband has disappeared. The Hanafi school requires a formal qadi's ruling of death before she can remarry, while the Maliki school typically allows remarriage after a fixed term (e.g., four years). The author proposes a legal stratagem (hila): after the Maliki waiting period, the wife re-petitions the qadi for a death ruling, thereby satisfying both schools. In dar al-harb, the Maliki position converges with the Hanafi, except for Ashhab, who treats the missing husband as a captive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
The wife of a missing husband (mafqud) cannot remarry because her husband's status is uncertain. The Hanafi school insists on certainty through a qadi's ruling, while the Maliki school sets a fixed period (e.g., four years) after which she may remarry without a formal ruling.
This uncertainty creates practical hardship for the wife.
2
State the Hanafi and Maliki positions
Hanafi: the wife must wait until all peers of the missing husband have died or a qadi rules his death. Maliki (majority): the wife may remarry after a fixed term (commonly four years) without a formal death ruling, following a narration from Umar.
The Maliki position is based on a specific precedent, but the Hanafi school requires a judicial determination.
3
Understand the hila (legal stratagem)
The hila is: after the four-year Maliki waiting period, the wife should re-petition the qadi to obtain a formal ruling of the missing husband's death. This ruling allows her to observe the iddat al-wafat (four months and ten days) and then remarry.
This satisfies both schools: the Maliki time condition and the Hanafi requirement for a qadi's ruling.
4
Examine the dar al-harb variation
In dar al-harb, the Maliki majority holds the same view as in dar al-Islam (wait until peers die), which coincides with the Hanafi position. However, Ashhab (a prominent Maliki) treats the missing person like a captive, prohibiting remarriage indefinitely until death is confirmed.
The hila may not be needed in dar al-harb if the Maliki view aligns with the Hanafi.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: in dar al-Islam, Hanafi requires a qadi's ruling, Maliki does not. The author recommends the cautious approach: always obtain the qadi's death ruling after the waiting period, to ensure both schools' conditions are met.
In dar al-harb where re-petition is difficult, the author allows acting without it.

Source Text

So he is deferred for four years (up to his statement) then she observes the waiting period of death and the maintenance is dropped, and she does not need permission. Al-Dardir said under it: without permission from the one who eats, because his permission was obtained by setting the term first, etc. And it comes in the seventh narration of the fatwa of Allamah Muhammad Tayyib ibn Ishaq, Mufti of the Malikis in Medina. But caution in this is that when the four years appointed by the qadi are completed, she should again apply and obtain from the qadi the ruling of the death of the missing person, so that the Hanafi school is observed as much as possible. But in a place where presenting the case again to the qadi etc. is very difficult, there is no harm in acting without re-presenting the case. 55. After waiting, the wife of the missing person should be given permission again: the reason is that according to the Hanafis, after the death of all the peers of the missing person, obtaining a ruling of his death is a condition, as narrated in Durr al-Mukhtar from Fatawa Qayyimah. 'Indeed, they rule his death by qada because the matter is probabilistic and unclear, and the hand of the qadi does not become a barrier.' And besides caution being required by the rules, this is also narrated from Umar: that upon the woman's second request, he gave a ruling of death of the missing person and ordered her to observe the waiting period of death. And the original condition of the Maliki school in the matter of the missing person is the ruling of Umar. Then it is unknown why they differ in this part. The second point: that even though the two eyes got a one-year respite from the qadi, the wife of the absent one had to re-apply after that one year. In this, the Malikis agree with the Hanafis. So it is unknown what difference they perceive between the absent one and the missing one. And Allah knows best. This ruling was in dar al-Islam. And in dar al-harb, the ruling for the wife of the missing person, according to the Malikis, is the same as according to the Hanafis: that as long as his peers are alive, there is no way for his wife to separate from the marriage and marry another, as is clear from the third narration of Allamah Sa'id bin Siddiq, Mufti of the Maliki school in Medina. And some scholars have specified its duration according to natural life expectancy, in which there are different statements: according to some, 70 years; according to some, 75 years; according to some, 80 years, etc. But it is better to entrust this matter to the people of knowledge; they should investigate and ascertain its defect. But Ashhab (who is among the distinguished students of Imam Malik and holds a high rank among the Maliki jurists) applied the same ruling to the wife of the missing person in dar al-harb as passed in dar al-Islam. As Ibn Rushd mentioned in his Muqaddimat (Mudawwana, vol. 2, p. 157): 'Discussion: As for the missing person in the lands of the Arabs, we apply the ruling of a captive: his wife does not marry and his wealth is not divided until his death becomes known or such time passes that one like him does not return, according to the saying of all our companions except Ashhab, for he ruled concerning the missing person in wealth and wife together, etc.' The period will start from when the qadi becomes hopeless. And the other underlined phrase is the translation of his Arabic phrase. (Qasimi) Answer to the question.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a comparative ruling on the wife of a missing husband (mafqud) and includes a legal stratagem (hila) to harmonize Hanafi and Maliki positions. The author begins by summarizing the earlier discussion: a delay of four years is set, after which the wife may observe the death-iddah and the maintenance obligation drops, with no need for permission from the missing husband's side. However, a cautionary hila is then proposed: after the four years appointed by the qadi, the wife should re-petition the qadi to obtain a formal death ruling for the missing husband. This ensures observance of the Hanafi requirement that the death of all peers (or a specific ruling) is necessary. The reason for this hila is that Hanafis condition the death ruling even after all peers have died, as cited from Durr al-Mukhtar and Fatawa Qayyimah, and it is also supported by a narration from Umar that the wife should obtain a death ruling. The author then criticizes the Maliki school for differing on this point, especially since they too follow Umar's practice in principle. A second issue highlights inconsistency: even in the case of an absent person (gha'ib) who is not missing, both schools agree that the wife must re-apply after a one-year respite, so why do they distinguish between the absent and the missing? The passage then moves to the ruling in dar al-harb (non-Muslim territory). According to the Maliki school (majority), the same ruling applies as in dar al-Islam: the wife cannot remarry until all peers die. However, some Malikis set a specific age limit (e.g., 70, 75, 80 years) after which the missing person is presumed dead. The author advises leaving the matter to experts. But Imam Ashhab, a prominent Maliki scholar, held a different view: in dar al-harb, the missing person is treated like a captive, so his wife cannot remarry and his wealth is not divided until his death is confirmed or a long time passes, aligning with the Hanafi position. Thus, the author presents a hila (obtaining a qadi's ruling) to allow the wife to remarry while satisfying both schools' conditions.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A person, specifically a husband, whose whereabouts are unknown and whose death is not confirmed.
دار الإسلام (dār al-islām): Territory where Islamic law is enforced and Muslims are safe.
دار الحرب (dār al-ḥarb): Territory not under Muslim rule, where there is no security for Muslims.
عدة الوفاة (ʿiddat al-wafāt): The waiting period of a widow: four months and ten days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary justification for the hila (obtaining a qadi's death ruling after the waiting period)?
A) The Maliki school requires a second petition.
B) The Hanafi school requires a formal death ruling as a condition for certain knowledge.
C) The missing husband's property cannot be divided without a ruling.
D) The qadi's ruling overrides the need for the iddah.

Key Takeaway
The hila of obtaining a qadi's death ruling after the waiting period ensures the wife's remarriage is valid according to both Hanafi and Maliki schools, reconciling their procedural differences.

Costs of Investigating a Missing Husband
Ruling on who bears investigation costs for a missing husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the different opinions on who bears the investigation costs for a missing husband and apply the preferred Hanafi view based on the wife's financial status.

Core Idea
The cost of investigating a missing husband falls on the wife if she is wealthy, otherwise on the public treasury or community.

When a husband goes missing, his wife may need to investigate his fate to seek divorce or inheritance. But who pays for the investigation? This passage records a difference among Hanafi jurists, with the preferred view depending on the wife's ability to pay. Understanding this rule is essential for muftis handling such cases, especially where no public treasury exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Recognize that the passage addresses a practical financial question in the context of a missing husband (mafqud): who bears the costs of investigation (taḥqīq)?
2
List the three positions
Extract the three opinions: (1) all costs on the wife, (2) all costs on bayt al-mal (public treasury), (3) detailed view: wife pays if she has wealth, else bayt al-mal.
The third view is attributed to a fatwa by 'Ala Marina Basham.
3
Note the preferred view
Observe that the text presents the detailed view as the one to follow, as it balances justice and practicality.
4
Address absence of bayt al-mal
Understand that where no public treasury exists (e.g., India), the government should bear the cost if possible, otherwise a collection from the Muslim community is recommended.
This shows adaptation to context.
5
Connect to the larger hila
Recognize that this ruling is part of the legal stratagem (hīla) to enable the wife to dissolve the marriage when the husband is missing.
The investigation cost is a procedural hurdle that must be resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مفقود کی تحقیق مفقود کی تنقید کے اخراجات کا کون ذمہ دار ہے ؟ اور تفتیش کے تمام اخراجات کے سلسلہ میں فقہاء میں اختلاف ہے بعض حضرات نے فرمایا یہ تمام اخراجات (مقدمہ دائر کرنے والی) عورت (مفقود کی بیوی ) کے ذمہ ہے اور بعض نے فرمایا بیت المال کے ذمہ ہے اور بعض کے نزدیک یہ تفصیل ہے کہ اگر بیوی کے پاس مال ہو تو تحقیق و تفتیش کے تمام اخراجات اُس کے ذمہ ہوں گے ورنہ بیت المال کے ذمہ جیسا کہ حضرت علا مارینا باشم کے فتوی میں نے روایت ہے اور جس جگہ بیت المال نہ ہو جیسے ہندوستان وغیرہ میں اگر ان مواقع میں حکومت اخراجات برداشت کر سے تو بہتر ہے ورنہ مسلمانوں سے چندہ کر لیا جائے۔
Who is responsible for the costs of investigation and scrutiny of the missing person? Regarding all expenses of investigation, there is disagreement among the jurists. Some said these all expenses are on the woman (the wife of the missing person). Some said they are on the public treasury. And according to some, there is a detail: if the wife has wealth, then all investigation expenses will be upon her; otherwise, on the public treasury, as is narrated in the fatwa of Hadrat 'Ala Marina Basham. And where there is no public treasury, like India etc., if in those situations the government bears the expenses, it is better; otherwise, collect donations from Muslims.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the financial responsibility for investigating a missing husband (mafqud). It first raises the question of who bears the costs, then records three positions among the fuqaha. The first position assigns all costs to the wife, the second assigns them to the public treasury (bayt al-mal), and the third (which the author seems to prefer) differentiates based on the wife's financial status: if she has wealth, she pays; if not, the public treasury bears the cost. The author then addresses the scenario where no public treasury exists (e.g., India), recommending that the government cover the costs if possible, otherwise a collection from Muslims should be made. This ruling is part of the larger hila (legal stratagem) for the wife of a missing husband to obtain a dissolution of marriage.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A person whose whereabouts are unknown and whose death cannot be confirmed.
بیت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Islamic state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, who bears the investigation costs if the wife of a missing husband is wealthy?
A) The wife
B) The public treasury
C) The husband's family
D) The government

Key Takeaway
The cost of investigating a missing husband is borne by the wife if she has means; otherwise, the community shoulders it.

Hila Shariyyah — Legal Stratagems
advanced
8 min
Ruling for a Missing Husband: Adopting the Maliki View as a Legal Stratagem in Dar al-Harb
Presents rulings on mafqud with Maliki and Hanafi views on jurisdiction and remarriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of hila to adopt the Maliki view for a mafqud case and state the conditions under which the wife may remarry after four years in Dar al-Harb.

Core Idea
A missing wife need not wait indefinitely: the Hanafi mufti may adopt the Maliki view as a legal stratagem when investigation is impossible.

This lesson examines the critical issue of a missing husband (mafqud) in non-Muslim territories. While the Hanafi school generally requires a very long wait (up to 120 years) or a judge's ruling, the Maliki position allows remarriage after four years of investigation. In this text from Al-Hilatul Najijah, we see how Hanafi muftis may adopt the Maliki view as a hila (legal stratagem) to relieve hardship, particularly in lands like India where thorough investigation is difficult.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Recognize that the case involves a missing husband (mafqud) in Dar al-Harb where Islamic symbols may be absent or investigation is limited. Understand that the wife seeks remarriage.
The text contrasts Dar al-Harb with Dar al-Islam; India is treated as Dar al-Islam for this ruling due to the presence of Islamic symbols.
2
Contrast the default Hanafi position with the Maliki position
State the Hanafi default: wife not divorced, wealth not inherited until very long period (e.g., 120 years) or a Muslim ruler's ruling. State the Maliki position: wife may remarry after four years if investigation is possible and husband likely dead.
The Hanafi position is based on preserving marriage until death is proven; the Maliki view allows earlier dissolution due to hardship.
3
Recognize the legal stratagem (hila)
Identify that the text adopts the Maliki view as a hila for Dar al-Harb scenarios. The hila is permissible because the default Hanafi position causes severe hardship and the Maliki view is a valid scholarly opinion.
Hila is a legal device to achieve a permissible outcome while adhering to the spirit of the law.
4
Apply the condition of current jurisdiction (wilayah)
Note that for the judge's decision to be binding, the judge must have current jurisdiction over both the wife and the missing husband (or at least the wife if the judge has general authority). Past jurisdiction does not suffice.
The text explicitly says 'current guardianship is a condition; past guardianship is not considered.'
5
Determine the practical outcome
Conclude that under the adopted Maliki view, the wife may remarry after four years of waiting and completing the iddat al-wafat (waiting period of death), provided that investigation is not possible or futile. The judge should have current wilayah over the case.
The text states: 'the wife of the missing person will be given the option to observe the waiting period of death and remarry after four years in those countries.'

لیکن مجنون یا عنین کی بیوی تنہا کسی قاضی کے علاقہ میں چلی جائے تو قاضی کا فیصلہ معتبر نہ ہوگا بلکہ یہ ضروری ہے کہ مجنون اور عنین بھی اس قاضی کے علاقہ میں ہوں۔ سوال کا جواب یہ ہے کہ حکم دار الحرب اور ہندوستان میں مفقود کا حکم دارالاسلام میں مثل ہے جیسا کہ سوال اول کے جواب میں تفصیلی طور پر گذر چکا۔ لیکن علماء مالکیہ کے فتاویٰ سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ ہندوستان، مصر، شام وغیرہ ممالک کہ جن میں کافر حکومت ہونے کے باوجود اسلامی شعائر تاحال قائم نہیں ان تمام میں مفقود کا حکم وہی ہے جو کہ دارالاسلام میں ہے۔ بلکہ جس دار الحرب میں اسلامی شعائر بھی موجود نہ ہوں اگر وہاں پر مسلمانوں کو صلح وغیرہ کی وجہ سے آنا جانا اور تفتیش کرنا ممکن ہو، اس دارالحرب میں بھی مفقود کا وہی حکم ہے جو کہ دارالاسلام میں ہے۔ پس اصل بنیاد مفقود کی غیر معمولی تحقیق و تفتیش ہے۔ اس وجہ سے ہندوستان کے دارالحرب ہونے میں جو علماء کا اختلاف ہے اس کا اس مسئلہ پر کوئی اثر نہیں پڑے گا اور مفقود کی بیوی کو ان ملکوں میں چار سال کے بعد عدت وفات گزار کر نکاح ثانی کا اختیار دیا جائے گا جیسا کہ علامہ الفا باشیم کی پانچویں روایت اور علامہ طیب کی ۲۵ ویں روایت میں ہے۔
گم شدہ شوہر کی ولایت کا مسئلہ (قدیم نسخہ کا حاشیہ کا مضمون)۔ اگر اس موقعہ پر کوئی شخص یہ شعبہ کرے کہ مفقود الخبر تخص جہاں کا باشندہ ہے مذہب مالکیہ کی اختیار کی گئی روایت: اما السوال الثاني وهل يلزم حكم الحاكم اوجماعة المسلمين بانتظار الاربع سنين اوصاح بلا حكم من حاكم فجوابه من شرح الدردير وحاشيته ان رفعها امرها للقاضي يجب فان رفعت والی...
ہے وہاں کے قاضی کی ولایت اگرچہ اس وقت تو اس پر ثابت نہیں ہے مگر اس سے پہلے اس پر ولایت ثابت تھی اس وجہ سے ولایت اصلیہ کی وجہ سے وہاں کے قاضی کا فیصلہ نافذ ہو سکتا ہے، اور جس قاضی کی ولایت میں شروع سے ہی نہیں تھا اس کا فیصلہ نافذ نہ ہونا چاہیے۔ تو اس بات کا جواب یہ ہے کہ قاضی کے فیصلہ کے نافذ ہونے کے لیے ولایت حال شرط ہے، سابقہ ولایت معتبر نہیں ہے۔ پس تمام جگہ کے قاضی... (بقیہ حاشیہ صفر گذشتہ کا) السياسة او والى الماء الحجاب الزكاة مع وجود القاضي حومه عليها ذلك وضم الحكم وان رفعت الجماعة المسلمين مع وجود القاضى بطل الحكوران لو يوجد قاضي خيرت في الرقع الوالي او الساعي فان رفعت الجماعة المسلمين مع وجود همانا نظاهر الحجة اما ان كانوا جائرين باخذ مال منها ظلما نيكشفوالها عن حال وجها فلها الرفع لجاعة المسلمين اما اجرت المبعوث لطلب الزوج فقيل على الزوجتي قبل على بيت المال وقيل ان كان لها مال فعليها بيت المال - وعنده الحنابلة لا يفتقر في ضرب المدة الى حاكو البلدة اهـ - فائدة عن المسئول عند زائدة عند الحنفية لا تطلق زوجة المفقود ولا يورث ما لا يورث ماله لا بعد سن المتعير مائة وعشرين أو تسمين او تمایدن او سبعین اوستین او برای حاكو المسلمين وعند الخاملة ان كان بظاهر غيبته السلامة لا تطلق امرأيه ولا تورث تركته الأمر تسعين سنة وان كانا ظاهرة الهلاك فبعد اربع سنين عند الشافعة في قول الشافعي القديم تطلق بعد اربع سنين ويورث بعد مدة لا يعيش الى مثلها و في الجديد لا تطليق ولا توريث الا بعد ثبوت موته او طلاقه لما رواه الشافعي عن على رضي الله عنه امرأة المفقود اقليت فلتصبر حتى يأتي بنتين موته والحديث امرأة المفقود امرأته حتى يا بيتها البيان رواه الدارقطني واليقي من المغيرة بن شعبة لكن الثقافية والحنابلة المايكية في جو انطليقها بعد النفقة رما خو دانه نهادی مالیکیه) ناقل خورشید حسن خانی
But if the wife of a madman or impotent man goes alone to a judge's territory, the judge's decision will not be valid; rather it is necessary that the madman and the impotent man also be in that judge's territory. The answer to the question is that the ruling of Dar al-Harb and India regarding a missing person is similar to the ruling in Dar al-Islam, as detailed in the answer to the first question. However, from the fatwas of the Maliki scholars it appears that in countries such as India, Egypt, Syria, etc., where despite non-Muslim rule Islamic symbols are not currently established, the ruling for a missing person is the same as in Dar al-Islam. Rather, even in a Dar al-Harb where Islamic symbols are not present, if Muslims can come and go and investigate due to peace treaties etc., the ruling for a missing person is the same as in Dar al-Islam. So the fundamental basis is extraordinary investigation and inquiry of the missing person. Because of this, the disagreement among scholars about India being Dar al-Harb has no effect on this issue, and the wife of the missing person will be given the option to observe the waiting period of death and remarry after four years in those countries, as stated in the fifth narration of Allama al-Fa Bashim and the 25th narration of Allama Tayyib. (The issue of the guardianship of the missing husband – content of the old copy's margin). If someone at this point raises the objection that the missing person is a resident of a specific place, and the adopted Maliki narration: ‘As for the second question, whether the ruling of a judge or a group of Muslims is binding regarding waiting four years or otherwise without a judge's ruling, the answer from Sharh al-Dardir and its gloss is: If she raises her matter to a judge, it is obligatory; if she raises it to a...’ There is the guardianship of the judge there; although at that time it is not established for him, but before that guardianship was established for him, so due to original guardianship the judge's decision can be enforced. And a judge for whom guardianship was not established from the beginning, his decision should not be enforced. The answer to this is that for the enforcement of a judge's decision, current guardianship is a condition; past guardianship is not considered. So all judges... (remainder of the margin from before) ‘al-siyasa aw wala al-ma' al-hijab al-zakat ma'a wujud al-qadi hawamahu 'alayha dhalik wa dam al-hukm wa in rafa'at al-jama'ah al-muslimin ma'a wujud al-qadi batala al-hukm wa in lam yujad qadi khayyirat fi al-raf' ila al-wali aw al-sa'i fa in rafa'at al-jama'ah al-muslimin ma'a wujud...’ Apparently the evidence; but if they are oppressors taking her wealth unjustly, they inform her of her situation, so she may raise it to the group of Muslims. As for the fee of the envoy sent to seek the husband, it is said it is upon the wife; it is said upon the treasury; and it is said if she has wealth, it is upon her treasury. And according to the Hanbalis, setting the period does not require a judge of the town. Benefit: Regarding the questioned, additional from the Hanafis: The wife of a missing person is not divorced, nor is his wealth inherited, not after the age of... one hundred twenty or ninety or eighty or seventy or sixty, or by a Muslim ruler. According to the... if the apparent of his absence is safety, his wife is not divorced nor is his estate inherited except after ninety years; and if there is apparent destruction, then after four years. According to the Shafi'is, in the old saying of al-Shafi'i, she is divorced after four years and inheritance after a period that he could not live to; in the new saying, no divorce or inheritance except after confirmation of his death or divorce, because al-Shafi'i narrated from ‘Ali, may Allah be pleased with him: ‘The wife of a missing person, let her wait until news of his death comes.’ And the hadith: ‘The wife of a missing person is his wife until evidence comes.’ Narrated by al-Daraqutni and al-Yaqi from al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba. But al-Thaqafiyya and al-Hanabila and al-Malikiyya permit her divorce after the expenses. (This is what is taken from the Maliki school) Narrated by Khurshid Hasan Khani.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hilatul Najijah addresses the complex issue of a missing husband (mafqud) and the conditions under which his wife may remarry. It begins by clarifying a procedural point: if a wife of a madman or impotent man goes alone to a judge in a different territory, the judge's decision is invalid unless the husband is also under that judge's jurisdiction. The main discussion then shifts to the ruling for mafqud in Dar al-Harb (non-Muslim territory) versus Dar al-Islam. The author states that the ruling in India is similar to Dar al-Islam, as detailed earlier. However, the Maliki scholars hold that even in countries like India, Egypt, and Syria where Islamic symbols are not fully established, the mafqud ruling is the same as in Dar al-Islam. Even if Islamic symbols are absent, if Muslims can investigate due to peace treaties, the same ruling applies. The core principle is thorough investigation of the missing person. Therefore, disagreements about whether India is Dar al-Harb do not affect this ruling; the wife may remarry after four years of waiting. The passage then presents an objection about the judge's jurisdiction: a person might claim that the judge of the missing person's original territory has prior guardianship, so his decision should be valid. The response is that current guardianship is required, not past. The text then quotes Maliki sources on the procedure when a judge exists or not. It concludes with a note on Hanafi and Shafi'i positions: Hanafis do not divorce the wife of a mafqud or distribute his wealth until a long period (up to 120 years) or a ruling; Shafi'is have two views: old (divorce after four years if destruction is apparent) and new (no divorce without proof of death). The adopted Maliki view permits divorce after the waiting period and expenses.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A missing person whose whereabouts and life status are unknown.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): A territory under non-Muslim rule where Islamic laws are not enforced.
ولایت (wilayah): Legal authority or jurisdiction of a judge.
حیلہ (hila): A legal stratagem or device to achieve a permissible outcome while adhering to the spirit of the law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maliki view adopted in this passage, when can the wife of a missing husband remarry in Dar al-Harb?
A) After 90 years of waiting
B) After four years of waiting and investigation
C) Only after the husband is confirmed dead
D) Immediately after one year of absence

Key Takeaway
In Dar al-Harb where investigation is impossible, a Hanafi mufti may adopt the Maliki view as a hila to allow the wife of a missing husband to remarry after four years and completing iddat al-wafat, provided the judge has current jurisdiction.

Two Scenarios for the Return of a Missing Person
Fatwa response on mafqud return scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two scenarios for the return of a missing person after being declared dead and apply the Hanafi ruling for each scenario.

Core Idea
If a missing person returns before consummation of the second marriage, he recovers his wife; after consummation, he loses her.

This passage from Al-Hilatul Najijah addresses a critical family law issue: what happens when a missing person (mafqud) returns after the qadi has declared him dead and his wife has remarried? The answer distinguishes two scenarios based on whether the second marriage has been consummated. Understanding these distinctions is essential for muftis dealing with modern cases of long-term disappearance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage describes two distinct scenarios: (1) the missing person returns before valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) with the second husband; (2) he returns after valid seclusion has occurred.
Valid seclusion in Hanafi fiqh has the same effect as actual intercourse regarding dower and iddah.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
For the first scenario, the wife remains with the first husband by consensus. For the second, the Maliki and Hanafi schools (and the preferred Shafi'i view as noted) hold that the wife stays with the second husband; the first husband has no claim.
The passage cites Maliki narrations as evidence.
3
Extract the Hanafi position
Although the Hanafi position is not explicitly stated in this passage, it aligns with the famous Maliki view: the second marriage is valid and the first marriage is dissolved by the qadi's ruling.
Hanafi sources: the qadi's ruling is binding; the wife observes iddat al-wafat and is free to remarry.
4
Analyze the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
A minority Maliki narration and some Shafi'i opinions hold that the second marriage becomes invalid (batil) upon the return. The passage notes this but gives more weight to the dominant view.
This difference affects the wife's status and the lineages of any children.
5
Apply the ruling to modern cases
In contemporary practice, if a person disappears and is declared dead after due process, and the spouse remarries and consummates, the second marriage is valid. If the first husband returns, he cannot reclaim his wife but may recover the dower if unpaid.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text

بیوی سے ہمبستری کرنے سے پہلے واپس آجائے تو مفقود کی بیوی اس کو واپس لے گی یا نہیں ؟ اور تمام صورتوں کا ایک ہی حکم ہے یا مختلف ؟ (۳) دوسرے شوہر سے صرف نکاح یا نکاح اور مباشرت [؟] دونوں ہونے کے بعد ؟ ب: تجدید نکاح کی صورت میں تجدید مہر دینی نے ہر بندھوانے کی بھی ضرورت ہے یا نہیں ؟ ج : اس صورت میں دوسرے شوہر کی مدت بھی واجب ہوگی یا نہیں ؟ اور اگر واجب ہوگی تو کتنے دن عورت کو مدت گذار نالازم ہوگی زمانہ عادت میں اولاد ہو جائے تو اس اولاد کا نسب کسی سے ثابت ہو گا ؟ پہلے شوہر سے یا دوسرے شوہر سے ؟ الجواب : مفقود کی واپسی کی دو صورتوں کا حکم وہ مفقود جس پر مقدمہ قائم کرنے یا تحقیق و تفتیش کے بعد چار سال تک انتظار کر کے قاضی نے موت کا حکم کر دیا ہے، اگر موت کا حکم ہونے کے بعد واپس آجائے تو اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں ۔ پہلی صورت یہ کہ دوسرے شوہر کے ساتھ خلوت صحیحہ ہونے سے پہلے پہلے آجائے چاہے عدت وفات کے بعد یا عدت وفات کے اندر اور چاہیے نکاح ثانی سے پہلے یا بعد اور دوسری صورت یہ کہ ایسے وقت واپس آئے جب کہ عدت وفات گزارنے کے بعد عورت دوسرے شخص سے نکاح کر چکی اور خلوت صحیحہ بھی ہو چکی اُن میں سے طیب بن اسحاق رفی کی رو سے مذکور ہے۔ اگر چهہ حضرت شعرانی نے میزان میں لکھا ہے۔ اور دوسری روایت میں ہے کہ بہر حال وہ عورت پہلے شوہر ہی کے پاس رہے گی اور حضرت امام شافعی سے راجح قول یہ ہے کہ دوسرا نکاح بال ہے ریعنی جبکہ مفقود واپس آجائے
Missing person (Rulings on the return of the person mentioned above). Question: If the missing person returns after being declared dead by the Shari committee or Shari panel, or returns before his wife marries another person or before the other person has intercourse with his wife, will the missing person's wife take him back? And is the ruling the same for all cases or different? (3) After only marriage or both marriage and intercourse [؟] with the second husband, upon the return of the missing person, if the wife meets him, the following questions arise: (a) Does the first husband need to remarry or will the first marriage be considered continuing and remaining? (b) In case of renewal of marriage, is it necessary to renew the dower and perform a new contract? (c) In this case, will the wife have to observe iddah for the second husband? And if so, for how many days must she observe iddah? And will this iddah be spent at the second husband's house or the first husband's? (3) Is the dower owed by the second husband still obligatory to pay? (e) If a child is born from the second husband, or if a child is born during the period of habit after separation, to whom will the lineage be attributed? To the first husband or the second? Answer: The ruling on the return of the missing person has two scenarios. The missing person for whom, after filing a case or investigation and inquiry, after waiting for four years, the qadi issued a ruling of death, if he returns after the ruling of death, there are two scenarios. First scenario: He returns before valid seclusion with the second husband, whether after the iddah of death or during the iddah of death, and whether before the second marriage or after. Second scenario: He returns when, after completing the iddah of death, the woman has married another person and valid seclusion has also occurred. For the first scenario, the ruling is unanimously that the wife remains in the marriage of the first husband; she cannot stay with the second husband, as is clear from 'Allamah Salih Maliki's fatwa narrations 14 and 15 and narration 24. For the second scenario, the famous Maliki school is that the wife stays with the second husband; the first husband has no connection with her anymore, as mentioned in the fifteenth, seventh, and twentieth narrations of 'Allamah Salih Maliki, according to 'Allamah Tayyib ibn Ishaq Rafi'. However, Hadhrat Shira'ni wrote in al-Mizan, and in another narration, in any case the woman stays with the first husband, and from Imam Shafi'i the preferred opinion is that the second marriage is invalid, meaning when the missing person returns.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a legal query (istifta') and response (jawab) concerning the return of a missing person (mafqud) after a qadi has declared him dead following a four-year wait. The author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, is presenting the issue from the book Al-Hilatul Najijah, which deals with legal stratagems. The query lists several sub-questions about the wife's status, the need for remarriage, dower, iddah, and lineage. The answer distinguishes two scenarios: (1) if the first husband returns before the second husband has had valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) with the wife, then the wife remains with the first husband by unanimous agreement; (2) if returns after valid seclusion, the Maliki school (which is cited as the basis for the answer) holds that the wife stays with the second husband. The passage also notes a contrary Maliki narration reported by Shira'ni and the Shafi'i opinion that the second marriage becomes invalid (batil). The Hanafi position is not explicitly stated here, but in Hanafi fiqh, the ruling is similar: the wife remains with the first husband if he returns before the second marriage is consummated, and if after consummation, the first husband has no right to her, though the second marriage is considered valid (not batil) and the first husband must pay a dower for the period he was absent. The passage serves as a comparative fiqh discussion, highlighting differences among schools. This is typical in fatwa works to present multiple views.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A person who has disappeared and whose status (alive or dead) is unknown, especially concerning the rulings on his wife's remarriage.
خلوت صحیحہ (khalwa sahiha): Valid seclusion between a married couple, which establishes the same legal effects as actual intercourse regarding dower and iddah.
عدت وفات (iddat al-wafat): The waiting period observed by a widow, which is four months and ten days, after which she may remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the unanimous ruling for the first scenario (return before valid seclusion)?
A) The wife stays with the first husband.
B) The wife stays with the second husband.
C) The wife chooses.
D) The marriage is automatically dissolved.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is whether the second marriage was consummated: before consummation, the first husband reclaims his wife; after consummation, the second marriage stands.

Hanafi Ruling on Missing Husband (Mafqud) and the Fate of a Second Marriage
Ruling on missing husband's return and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a missing husband returns after his wife's remarriage and explain the legal consequences for both husbands, including the payment of mahr al-mithl and observance of iddah.

Core Idea
A missing husband's return automatically voids the second marriage; the wife returns to the first even after consummation.

This passage addresses a delicate family law scenario: a woman, after a prolonged waiting period for her missing husband, remarries. Then the first husband returns. What happens? The Hanafi school, drawing from the practice of Ali ibn Abi Talib and Imam Abu Hanifa, provides a definitive ruling that the second marriage is void ab initio. We will analyze the ruling step by step, understand the dispute with the view attributed to Umar, and see how the Hanafi position is relied upon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its source
The passage cites Mizan al-Shar'ani quoting Imam Abu Hanifa. Identify the exact ruling: the second marriage is void (batil) and the woman returns to her first husband.
This is the core Hanafi position.
2
Note the two sub-cases
Distinguish between (a) the second husband did not consummate the marriage, and (b) the second husband consummated. In the latter case, he must pay mahr al-mithl and the wife must observe iddah before returning to the first husband.
These consequences protect the woman's rights.
3
Analyze the dispute
The text mentions a disagreement between Ali and Umar, with Ibrahim al-Nakha'i preferring Ali's view. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: different outcomes for the marital status.
The Hanafi school adopts Ali's view.
4
Apply the conditions for the wife's return
The passage explains that even if the second husband had valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha), the wife remains with the first husband after completing iddah. She must observe iddah at the first husband's home, and he cannot approach her until iddah ends.
This is similar to a case of doubtful intercourse.

وقد قع رجوعه (يعني عمر الى قبول على خانه اى عليا كان يقول تود الى زوجها الأول و يفرق بينها وبين الاخر ولها المهربها استحل من مرجها ولا يقرتها الأول حتى تنقضى عدتها و من الأخر و بهذا كان يأخذ ابراهيم فيقول قول على احب الي من قول عمر و به ناخذ ايضا من جه وفي ميزان الشعراني ملاج " ومن ذالك تقول إلى حنفية ان المفقود اذا قد هر بعد ان تزوجت زوجة بعد التربص ويبطل العقد وهى الاول وان كان الثاني وطها فعليه مهر المثال وتعقد الثاني شوترد الى الاول الخ مفقود کی بیوی کے نکاح ثانی کے بعد اگر مفقود آجائے ترجمہ وحاصل عبارت بالا یہ ہے کہ میزان الشعرانی ۱۳ ج ۲ میں ہے کہ حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ ص مفقود جب واپس آئے جبکہ اُس کی بیوی نے نکاح ثانی کر لیا ہو تو اس کا دوسرا نکاح باطل ہو جائے گا اور عقد باطل ہو جائے گا اور عورت پہلے شوہر کو ملے گی۔ اور اگر اُس عورت کے دوسرے شوہر نے عورت سے ہمبستری کر لی ہو۔ تو اُس پر مر مثل اور عورت دوسرے شخص سے جدا ہو کہ) عدت گزار سے اور پھر وہ پہلے شوہر کو واپس ملے گی ۔
حنفی کو غیر حنفی مذہب اختیار کرنا
اور حنفی کے لیے غیر حنفیہ کے مذہب پر فتویٰ دینا سخت ضرورت کے وقت جائز ہے۔ جیسے مفقود کی بیوی کو مہلت وغیرہ کی صورتیں ، لیکن مفقود کی واپسی کی صورت میں دوسرے مذہب پر عمل کرنے کی کوئی ضرورت موجود نہیں ۔ اس وجہ سے اس دوسری صورت میں بھی ریعنی جبکہ مفقود کی واپسی سے سے پہلے دوسرا شوہر خلوت صحیحہ بھی کر چکا ہو جب بھی ہیوی اپ نے پہلے شوہر کے ہی نکاح میں رہے گی دوسرے شوہر کے پاس رہنا جائز نہیں کیونکہ پہلے شوہر کی واپسی سے دوسرا نکاح باطل قرار دیا گیا۔
(۲) پہلے سوال کے جواب میں مسور کی جو عبارت درج کی گئی ہے اس سے اس سوال کے پانچوں اجزاء کا جواب ہو گیا۔
مفقود کا پہلا نکاح باقی رہے گا مطلب یہ ہے کہ پہلا نکاح قائم رہے گا دوسرا نکاح کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے اگر چہ دوسرے شوہر سے عورت کی ہمبستری بھی ہو چکی ہو اور یہ بات قول تری چه الى زوجها الاول اور قول ولا يقرتها الاول سے مستفاد ہوتی ہے ۔ (ب) تظاہر ہے کہ جب دوسرے نکاح کی ضرورت نہیں تو پھر نیا ہر مقریہ کرنے کی کیا ضرورت رہی ؟ (ج) دوسرے شوہر کی عترت گذارنا واجب ہے جب تک کہ عدت ختم نہ ہو۔ اس وقت تک، پہلے شوہر کو اُس عورت کے پاس جانا ہرگز جائزہ نہیں ہے بلکہ پوری احتیاط لازم ہے۔ یہ مسئلہ ولا یقر بها الاول حتی يقرها تنقضی عد تھا من الاخر سے اور عدت میں جو تفصیل دوسرے مواقع میں ہے وہ یہاں بھی ہوگی لینی اگر عورت حاملہ ہے تو بچہ پیدا ہونے تک ورز تین حیض ۔ باقی یہ سوال کہ زمانہ عدت کس جگہ گزارے، تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کسی کہ عدت پہلے شوہر کے یہاں گزارے گی ، کیونکہ یہ عورت ایسی عورت کی طرح ہو گئی کہ جس سے شعبہ میں ہمبستری کر لی گئی ہو جیسا کہ شمس الائمہ نے فرمایا ہے صحیح یہ ہے کہ وہ عورت پہلے شوہر کی بیوی ہے لیکن پہلا شو ہر د عدت کے واقعد)
And his return occurred (meaning Umar accepted Ali's view? ... Ali used to say: she is returned to her first husband and separation is made between her and the second, and she has the option to [؟], and the first does not keep her until her iddah from the second expires. Ibrahim adopted this and said: Ali's statement is more beloved to me than Umar's statement, and we also adopt it from the angle. And in Mizan al-Shar'ani: 'And among that is the Hanafi statement: the missing person, when he returns after his wife has married after waiting, the marriage contract is invalid and she is for the first, even if the second had intercourse with her, then upon him is the mahr al-mithl, and the second marriage is annulled, and she is returned to the first.' Missing: When the missing person returns after his wife's second marriage: translation and summary of the above statement: In Mizan al-Shar'ani vol. 13, it is narrated that Imam Abu Hanifa said: When the missing person returns after his wife has contracted a second marriage, then her second marriage becomes void and the contract is invalid, and the woman goes to the first husband. And if the second husband had intercourse with her, then upon him is mahr al-mithl, and the woman separates from the second person and observes iddah, then she returns to the first husband.
Permissibility of a Hanafi adopting a non-Hanafi madhhab: And for a Hanafi to give fatwa based on a non-Hanafi madhhab in severe necessity is permissible, like the cases of the missing person's wife being given reprieve, etc. But in the case of the missing person's return, there is no need to act on another madhhab. For this reason, in that second case, i.e., when the missing person's return occurs before the second husband had valid seclusion, even then the wife remains in the marriage of the first husband; it is not permissible for her to stay with the second husband because the second marriage is deemed void due to the return of the first husband.
(2) In the answer to the first question, the text of Miswar that was entered answers all five parts of the question. The first marriage of the missing person will remain, meaning the first marriage continues and there is no need for a second marriage even if intercourse occurred with the second husband. This is derived from the statement 'returned to her first husband' and the statement 'the first does not keep her'. (b) It is apparent that when there is no need for a second marriage, then what need is there for a new marriage? (c) Observing the iddah of the second husband is obligatory until the iddah ends. During this time, it is not at all permissible for the first husband to approach that woman; rather full caution is necessary. This issue is from 'the first does not keep her until she completes her iddah from the second', and the details of iddah that are in other places apply here too, i.e., if the woman is pregnant then until childbirth, otherwise three menstruations. As for the question of where to spend the period of iddah, the answer is that she will spend her iddah at the first husband's home, because this woman has become like a woman with whom intercourse occurred in a doubtful marriage, as Shams al-A'imma said: the correct view is that she is the wife of the first husband, but the first husband cannot keep her until the iddah is over.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a hila case addressing the situation of a missing husband (mafqud) and the permissibility for a Hanafi to resort to a non-Hanafi madhhab. The author first cites a report from Mizan al-Shar'ani that Imam Abu Hanifa ruled: if a missing husband returns after his wife has remarried following a waiting period (tarabbus), the second marriage is automatically invalidated and the woman reverts to her first husband. If the second husband had intercourse, he must pay mahr al-mithl (the customary dower), and the woman must observe iddah from him before returning to the first husband. This is the Hanafi position, also attributed to Ali ibn Abi Talib and preferred by Ibrahim al-Nakha'i over Umar's view. The author then discusses a legal stratagem: it is permissible for a Hanafi mufti to give fatwa based on a non-Hanafi madhhab in cases of severe necessity, such as allowing the wife of a missing person to remarry after a certain period. However, once the missing husband returns, there is no need to rely on another madhhab because the Hanafi ruling itself resolves the matter. The passage elaborates: even if the second husband had valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha), the wife remains with the first husband. The iddah from the second husband must be observed, and during that time the first husband cannot have relations with her. The iddah details follow the usual rules: if pregnant, until delivery; otherwise three menstrual cycles. The woman must observe iddah in the first husband's home, as she is his wife, but consummation is prohibited until iddah ends, similar to a case of doubtful intercourse (shubha). The passage also responds to a five-part question, clarifying that the first marriage remains intact, no new marriage is needed, and iddah is obligatory.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A person whose whereabouts are unknown and whose life or death is not established
تربص (tarabbus): The waiting period after which the wife of a missing person may seek annulment or remarriage
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after separation from her husband before remarrying
خلوت صحیحہ (khalwa sahiha): Valid seclusion between a married couple that is equivalent to intercourse in some rulings
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The customary dower equivalent to what a woman of her status would receive

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a missing husband returns after his wife has remarried, what happens to the second marriage?
A) The second marriage remains valid if the second husband had intercourse.
B) The second marriage is automatically void, and the wife returns to the first husband.
C) The wife may choose between the two husbands.
D) The second marriage requires a fresh contract to be valid.

Key Takeaway
A missing husband's return automatically voids any subsequent marriage of his wife, reinforcing the primacy of the original marriage bond.

Hila Shariyyah — Legal Stratagems
intermediate
7 min
Permissibility for a Hanafi to Follow Another Madhhab in Severe Necessity
Permissibility of following another madhhab in severe necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a Hanafi mufti may legitimately adopt a non-Hanafi position and apply the condition of severe necessity to the case of a missing person's wife.

Core Idea
A Hanafi mufti may issue a fatwa based on another madhhab only when severe necessity exists, not as a default.

This passage extends the discussion on the missing husband to a wider principle: the permissibility for a Hanafi to adopt the ruling of another madhhab (taqlid shakhsi) when severe necessity demands it. The specific example given is the wife of a missing person who needs to remarry after a prolonged waiting period. However, the author emphasizes that once the necessity is removed (as when the husband returns), the Hanafi ruling must be applied and no reliance on other madhhabs is justified. Let us explore this important principle of hanafi fatwa methodology.

Guided Path
1
State the general principle
The passage states: 'And for a Hanafi to give fatwa based on a non-Hanafi madhhab in severe necessity is permissible.' This is a clear statement of the rule.
This is a form of legal stratagem (hila) to avoid hardship.
2
Identify the example of necessity
The text mentions the case of the missing person's wife being given reprieve, i.e., allowing her to remarry after a waiting period (tarabbus) according to another madhhab when the Hanafi waiting period is extremely long (up to 90-120 years).
Other madhhabs have shorter waiting periods, providing relief.
3
Recognize the limitation: necessity ends when the husband returns
The passage stresses that once the husband returns, there is no need to act on another madhhab; the Hanafi ruling itself resolves the matter. The wife must return to the first husband regardless of whether the second husband had seclusion.
Necessity is temporary; the ruling of the original madhhab revives.
4
Apply the condition to a fatwa scenario
A mufti must assess whether severe necessity (darurah) exists. If it does, he may give a fatwa according to another madhhab. If not, he must stick to the Hanafi position.
This prevents abuse of the permission.

Key Terms
ضرورت (darurah): Severe necessity that justifies deviation from ordinary rulings
تقليد شخصي (taqlid shakhsi): Following a particular madhhab on a specific issue, as opposed to madhhab-switching

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when is it permissible for a Hanafi mufti to give a fatwa based on a non-Hanafi madhhab?
A) Whenever the mufti finds the Hanafi ruling inconvenient.
B) Only in cases of severe necessity, such as the missing person's wife needing to remarry.
C) Only if the seeker specifically requests a non-Hanafi fatwa.
D) Never; a Hanafi must always give fatwa according to his own madhhab.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti may adopt another madhhab only in severe necessity, and must revert to the Hanafi ruling when the necessity ends.

Consequences of the Missing Husband's Return: The Hila Stratagem
Explaining the legal consequences when a missing husband returns after his wife has married another man.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the return of a missing husband to a case where the wife has remarried and had valid seclusion, and identify the rights and obligations of each party.

Core Idea
The second marriage invalidates automatically; the wife must return to the first husband after her iddah from the second.

This passage addresses a delicate legal scenario: a missing husband (mafqud) returns after his wife has remarried and even consummated the second marriage. The Hanafi school provides a precise ruling that balances the wife's need for clarity, the first husband's rights, and the second husband's obligations. We will dissect the consequences step by step, focusing on the marriage status, waiting period, and financial dues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parties and Sequence of Events
List the individuals involved: the first husband (mafqud), the wife, and the second husband. Note the key events: first husband disappears, wife remarries after court permission or passage of time, second husband enters valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha), then first husband returns.
Khalwa sahiha is treated like actual intercourse in Hanafi law.
2
Determine the Status of the Second Marriage
Apply the ruling: upon the first husband's return, the second marriage becomes invalid (bāṭil) automatically. The wife must leave the second husband immediately and return to the first husband. This holds even if consummation occurred.
The text states: 'the second marriage is invalidated by the first husband's return.'
3
Determine the Waiting Period and Where It Is Spent
The wife must observe an iddah from the second husband: three menstrual cycles (or until delivery if pregnant). She spends this iddah in the first husband's house because the first marriage is still valid. During iddah, the first husband cannot have conjugal relations because the wife is 'mu'qadat al-ghayr' (contracted to another) – a protective measure.
The wife may adorn herself for the first husband but must not engage in intimacy until iddah ends.
4
Determine the Dower Owed
If valid seclusion occurred, the second husband must pay the full stipulated dower (mahr musamma) to the wife. The text supports this with the principle that 'use of the private part necessitates the full dower.'
There is a difference of opinion: some say mahr al-mithl (dower of equivalence), but the text favors mahr musamma.

له ودواعيه ملحقة به كما هو الظاهر ۱۲ منه عمه ای تنظر كذا في القاموس ١٢ منه سے اور عرصہ درانہ کی تعمین معرض الی رای الحاکو لینی قاضی یا جماعت مسلمان مدعیہ کے خاص حالات میں غور کر کے قرار دیں کہ مقدمہ پیش ہونے سے بیشتر اس نے کافی انتظار کر لیا ہے یا نہیں اگر معمولی انتظار کے بعد مقدمہ دائر کر دیا تب تو احکام گذشتہ کے موافق چار سال کے مزید انتظار کا حکم دیا جاوے

If before (the first husband's return) the second husband had even performed valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha), then she will remain in the marriage of the first husband; it is not permissible for her to stay with the second husband because the second marriage is invalidated by the first husband's return. (2) The answer to the first question's five parts is covered by the quotation from Masur earlier. The first marriage of the missing husband remains—meaning the first marriage continues and there is no need for a second marriage, even if intercourse occurred with the second husband; this is derived from the statement "he will not retain her first" (la yuqirruha al-awwal). (b) It is apparent that when there is no need for a second marriage, what need remains for (the wife) to make a new declaration? (c) It is obligatory to spend on the second husband until the waiting period ends. During this time, the first husband is absolutely not permitted to approach that woman; rather, full caution is necessary. This issue is taken from "he will not retain her first until he retains her" and "her waiting period from the other expires." The details of waiting period that exist in other places apply here too: if the woman is pregnant, until childbirth; otherwise, three menses. As for the question of where to spend the waiting period, the answer is that she will spend it at the first husband's house, because this woman has become like a woman with whom intercourse occurred in shubha (doubt). As Shams al-A'imma stated: the correct view is that she is the wife of the first husband, but the first husband should not go near that woman during the waiting period, because she is mu'qadat al-ghayr (lit. contracted to another), like a married woman with whom intercourse has occurred in a city. (From Basut 2:2). And in Durar wa Ghurar, chapter of marriage, it says: "The woman who has been touched (by second husband) may stay with her first husband and go out with his permission during the waiting period because the marriage between them remains valid; there is no prohibition..." And al-Shami narrated from Kafi: "If a man's wife marries another and (the second) husband enters her, then they are separated and she returns to her first husband, she may adorn herself for her first husband and beautify for him, and she owes the waiting period of the other—three menses." And Allah knows best. End of section on haddad (page 1025). (3) If valid seclusion occurred, then the full dower that was fixed at the time of marriage must be paid. This is derived from his statement: "and for her is the dower because her private part became permissible," although it is not explicit whether the intended dower is the stipulated dower (mahr musamma) or the dower of equivalence (mahr al-mithl). However, the immediate understanding when unqualified is the stipulated dower. Also, what passed in the case of the insane person—that if the marriage is annulled after entry, the stipulated dower is due—supports what we say. And what is in al-Mizan al-Sha'rani (that he owes dower of equivalence) is not supported by any narration nor by legal principles; the understanding thereof is subject to discussion. The apparent meaning of al-Mabsut and al-Bada'i contradicts it, as we mentioned earlier. And Allah knows best. (From the commentary) and its ramifications are annexed to it, as is apparent. 12 from him, etc. Consider thus in al-Qamus. And from his age, the determination of a reasonable period is left to the view of the judge or a group of Muslims: they should decide based on the specific circumstances of the claimant whether she has waited long enough before filing the case. If she filed the case after only a short wait, then the ruling is as before—she must wait an additional four years. And if she has waited sufficiently and then filed the case, then according to this concession, a judgment is permitted.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage from al-Hilat al-Najijah addresses the legal stratagem (hila) surrounding the return of a husband who was missing (mafqud) after his wife had married another man. The passage presents the Hanafi ruling in a stepwise manner:

1. The second marriage is automatically invalidated upon the first husband's return, even if the second husband had valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha). The woman remains in the nikah of the first husband and must leave the second husband immediately. This is based on the principle that the first marriage was never dissolved.

2. The wife must observe a waiting period (iddah) from the second husband. During this iddah, the first husband cannot approach her because she is considered mu'qadat al-ghayr (a woman whose contract is with another), akin to a woman with whom intercourse occurred in a doubtful situation (shubha). The iddah is three menstrual periods unless she is pregnant, in which case it lasts until delivery.

3. The iddah is to be spent in the house of the first husband, since she is still his wife. She may adorn herself for him, but he must not have conjugal relations until the iddah expires.

4. If the second husband had valid seclusion, the full stipulated dower (mahr musamma) is due from him to the wife. The passage notes a difference of opinion: some say it's the stipulated dower, others say the dower of equivalence (mahr al-mithl). The author supports the stipulated dower, citing the principle that use of the private part necessitates the full dower, and references supporting views from al-Mabsut and al-Bada'i.

5. Finally, the passage discusses the waiting period before the wife can file a case for the missing husband's return. The judge (or a Muslim committee) must consider whether she has waited a reasonable time. If she filed too soon, she must wait an additional four years. If she waited sufficiently, the judge can grant a ruling according to the concession (i.e., declare the missing husband dead and allow remarriage).

Thus, the hila ensures the woman is not left in limbo while preserving the rights of both husbands and maintaining the integrity of marriage contracts.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A husband who has gone missing and whose whereabouts are unknown, and whose death is not confirmed.
خلوت صحیحہ (khalwa sahiha): Valid seclusion between a husband and wife in a place where intercourse is physically possible and no legal or physical barrier exists.
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after separation from her husband or death of the husband before she can remarry.
مہر مسما (mahr musamma): The dower specified in the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a missing husband returns after his wife has married another man and had valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) with him, what happens to the second marriage?
A) The second marriage remains valid because khalwa sahiha confirms it.
B) The second marriage is invalidated and the wife returns to the first husband.
C) The wife may choose which husband to stay with.
D) The second marriage is suspended until a judge decides.

Key Takeaway
Upon return of the missing husband, the second marriage is void, the wife must complete iddah from the second husband at the first husband's home, and the second husband owes the stipulated dower.

Hila Shariyyah — Legal Stratagems

8 min
The Stratagem for the Wife of a Missing Husband: Following the Maliki School under Necessity
Legal stratagem for the wife of a missing husband using the Maliki school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa applying the Maliki stratagem for the wife of a missing husband, identifying the conditions and legal consequences.

Core Idea
When hardship is certain, the Maliki fixed period may be followed, but sincerity and caution are essential.

In the Hanafi madhhab, the wife of a missing husband must wait indefinitely until his death is confirmed or he returns. This preserves the marriage but can cause severe hardship. The Maliki school offers a concession: after a fixed absence (four years, or one year under extreme fear), a judge may decree separation. This lesson unpacks the conditions, legal effects, and the proper use of this stratagem in fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi default
Identify the Hanafi rule: no fixed waiting period; the wife must wait indefinitely until the husband is proven dead or returns. The qadi cannot annul the marriage without certainty.
This preserves marriage but can cause hardship.
2
Identify the Maliki alternative
Recognize the Maliki position: the qadi may decree divorce after a fixed absence (four years, or one year if fear of sin). This is based on istihsan and public interest.
The alternative is a concession (rukhsa).
3
Examine the conditions for applying the stratagem
The stratagem is valid only when: the wife sincerely fears falling into sin, has waited a long time, and has exhausted patience. She must apply to a qadi. The fear must be certain, not speculative.
Without these conditions, applying the Maliki ruling is a pretext.
4
Determine the legal consequences
The separation is a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i). The wife observes three menstrual cycles as iddah. If the husband returns during iddah and reclaims her, the marriage continues. After iddah, the divorce becomes irrevocable.
This differs from iddah of death (4 months 10 days).

اگر تفریق (فسخ نکاح) اس قانون کے مطابق کیا جائے تو اس بات کا خیال ضروری ہے کہ یہ تفریق طلاق رجعی ہوگی اور اس صورت میں مفقود کی بیوی کو عدت وفات گزارنے کے بجائے طلاق کی عدت تین حیض گزارنے ہوں گے اور اگر مفقود اس صورت میں واپس آگیا تو اس میں یہ تفصیل ہوگی کہ اگر عدت کے دوران رجعت کرے تو رجعت [؟] صحیح ہو جائے گی اور بیوی بدستور اُس کے نکاح میں رہے گی اور اگر عدت [؟] کے بعد مفقود آگیا یا پہلے ہی آگیا لیکن عدت کے اندر قولی یا فعلی رجعت [؟] نہ کی تو اب مفقود کی بیوی پر طلاق بائنہ ہو کر وہ عورت خود مختار ہو گی خواہ وہ عورت دوبارہ اُسی شخص سے نکاح کرے یا کسی دوسرے شخص سے جیسا کہ علامہ صالح تونسی کی روایت میں مذکور ہے۔
زوجہ مفقود کی مدت انتظار کس سے شمار ہوگی؟
لیکن یہ بات کہ یہ سال مفقود کے غائب ہونے کے وقت سے شمار کیا جائے گا یا قاضی (یا شرعی کمیٹی) میں عورت کے مقدمہ دائر کرنے کے وقت سے شمار ہوگا تو فتاوی مالکیہ (جہاں سے عملہ لیا گیا ہے) میں مذکور نہیں ہے اور مالکی مذہب کی جس قدر کتب یہاں موجود تھیں ان میں بھی نہیں مل سکی، اور ظاہر ہے کہ احتیاط اسی میں ہے کہ مقدمہ پیش کرنے سے انتظار کا سال شمار کیا جائے۔
کے قدیم نسخہ میں ہی ضمون حاشیہ پر تھا جس کو بعنوان بالا پیش کیا گیا ہے (خورشید حسن قاسمی)
The lineage of this child will be established from the second husband. As clarified by Allamah al-Shami in Radd al-Muhtar (Bab al-Mafqud, page 361, volume 3), and the Malikis also agree, as stated in the thirty-third narration of the fatwa of Allamah al-Salih al-Maliki appended to the end of the book.

The ruling regarding the wife of a missing husband who does not live with chastity: in that case, it is unanimously necessary that the woman be able to endure with patience and chastity for such a duration. But if this is not possible – that is, the woman expresses fear of falling into sin, and she, having waited for the missing husband for a long period, submits a request in a state of compulsion, having become [؟] – then in that situation there is also allowance to reduce the period of four years in accordance with the Maliki school, because when there is severe fear of the woman committing sin, according to them, after at least one year [؟], separation (judicial annulment of marriage) is permissible, as mentioned in the second narration of the Maliki mufti Allamah al-[؟]. But the scholars of Saharanpur in both cases stipulate waiting the full four-year period, and this is obviously more precautionary. However, in a place where strong circumstantial evidence indicates a grave fear of the woman committing adultery, there is also allowance for the judge to act on the opinion of the one-year wait. But the matter is with the Holy Lord; no pretext should be sought. (Ashraf Ali)

If the separation (annulment) is carried out according to this ruling, it is necessary to consider that this separation will be a revocable divorce. In that case, the wife of the missing husband will observe the waiting period of three menstruations instead of the waiting period for death. If the missing husband returns in this situation, the detail is as follows: if he takes her back during the waiting period, the return [؟] will be valid, and the wife will remain in his marriage. But if the missing husband returns after the waiting period [؟], or even earlier but did not effect a verbal or actual return [؟] during the waiting period, then the wife becomes divorced with a irrevocable divorce, and she is free to marry again, whether to the same person or to another, as mentioned in the narration of Allamah Salih al-Tunisi.

From when will the waiting period of the missing husband's wife be counted?
But regarding whether this year is counted from the time of the husband's disappearance or from the time the woman files a case with the judge (or the Shariah committee), it is not mentioned in the Maliki fatawa (from which the practice is taken), nor could it be found in the available Maliki books. And evidently, the precaution is to count the waiting year from the filing of the case.

It was only in the old copy of the commentary on the margin that was presented under the above title. (Khurshid Hasan Qasimi)
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a hila (legal stratagem) addressing the predicament of a woman whose husband has been missing for an extended period. In the Hanafi madhhab, the wife must wait indefinitely until the husband either returns or is legally declared dead after a thorough investigation by the qadi. There is no fixed four-year period; the declaration of death typically requires evidence or a long wait (e.g., until the husband would be 90 years old). However, recognizing the hardship and potential sin, the text presents the Maliki option as a valid stratagem.

The Maliki madhhab allows a qadi to decree a divorce after a fixed period of absence—generally four years, and in some narrations, as little as one year if there is a strong fear of spiritual or moral harm. The text notes that the scholars of Saharanpur (a Hanafi center) prefer the four-year period as more precautionary, but permit the one-year ruling when there is clear evidence that the wife is at risk of adultery. This illustrates the Hanafi principle of 'necessity permits exceptions' (al-darurah tubih al-mahzurat).

The stratagem operates as follows: the wife presents her case to a judge who can apply the Maliki ruling. The resulting separation is considered a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i), so the waiting period is three menstrual cycles (iddah of divorce) rather than four months and ten days (iddah of death). If the missing husband returns during this iddah, he may reclaim her without a new marriage. If the iddah expires without his return, the divorce becomes irrevocable (bainuna), and she is free to marry another.

The text also addresses the start of the waiting period: whether from the husband's disappearance or from filing the case. Since Maliki sources are silent on this, the author advises caution by counting from the filing date, as it is more precise and avoids backdating.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A person who has disappeared and whose whereabouts are unknown
تفریق (tafriq): Judicial separation or dissolution of marriage
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of divorce does the judicial separation (tafriq) for the wife of a missing husband become?
A) Talaq bain (irrevocable divorce)
B) Talaq raj'i (revocable divorce)
C) Faskh nikah (annulment)
D) Talaq sunni

Key Takeaway
The Maliki stratagem for the wife of a missing husband is permissible only under genuine fear of sin and with careful observation of the iddah and return rules.
